<div id="review_219896104" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/219896104" />
    <a title="Kirsten" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/821868-kirsten"><img alt="Kirsten" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1205553862p2/821868.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/219896104?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kirsten" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kirsten" href="/user/show/821868-kirsten">Kirsten</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/821868-kirsten?shelf=pacific-northwest">pacific-northwest</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/821868-kirsten?shelf=children-or-ya">children-or-ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/821868-kirsten?shelf=from-library">from-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/821868-kirsten?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/821868-kirsten?shelf=spec-fic">spec-fic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer219896104" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10937997961088769811">Meh. I really liked the concept here: a sort of Alternate Portland with an Impassible Wilderness in St. John's, with the St. John's bridge as a ghostly portal. But I was pretty disappointed with the execution.<br/><br/>The language is often kind of overwritten and clunky. Prue and Curtis are not well-defined as characters; I wasn't even able to put my finger on how old Prue was supposed to be until she came right out and said she was twelve at one point. First I thought she was much younger, if precocious</span>
  <span id="freeText10937997961088769811" style="display:none">Meh. I really liked the concept here: a sort of Alternate Portland with an Impassible Wilderness in St. John's, with the St. John's bridge as a ghostly portal. But I was pretty disappointed with the execution.<br /><br />The language is often kind of overwritten and clunky. Prue and Curtis are not well-defined as characters; I wasn't even able to put my finger on how old Prue was supposed to be until she came right out and said she was twelve at one point. First I thought she was much younger, if precocious, but couldn't quite buy that if she was eight or nine she'd be babysitting her brother all day, unless her parents were of the negligent type. It almost felt as though her age changed throughout. Similarly, I had a hard time believing that, if Curtis was twelve, he would have been rejected by his classmates for his "babyish" habit of drawing superheroes -- from my recollection, 11 and 12-year-olds who can do a decent drawing of Wolverine or Spiderman receive a grudging respect for their talents, even if they're low on the totem pole.<br /><br />Basically, the first thing a children's or YA author has to do with a child protagonist is establish them believably as a child, if a potentially heroic one, and Meloy fails to do so.<br /><br />The frustrating thing about this book is that if you describe the individual elements to almost anyone who loves books of this type, their eyes will light up: children kidnapped by crows, disappearing bridges, bandits, evil sentient ivy, an Avian Protectorate, etc. etc. Yet it's all a bit hollow and unsatisfying, in the end. I really feel like this is a book that needed to undergo at least one more major rewrite before it was published, and I'm sad that it didn't, because I think it could have been much, much better.<br /><br />On the other hand, I loved Carson Ellis's illustrations. She is AWESOME.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10937997961088769811" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/251736861?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessie" href="/user/show/71038-jessie">Jessie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71038-jessie?shelf=couldnotfinish">couldnotfinish</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer251736861" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9661127417704857327">Fun fact: When my husband was a child he was in a community theater play with Colin Meloy. Also, I listened to a lot of Tarkio in college in Montana and love the Decemberists. So I came to this as a fan of Meloy and was excited to hear his take on an "American Narnia without all the Jesus" as I've heard it described.<br/><br/>But ye gods, what a snoozefest this turned out to be. I hated, hated, hated the main characters. I know this is supposed to be a fantasy, but seriously. What upper middle class Portl</span>
  <span id="freeText9661127417704857327" style="display:none">Fun fact: When my husband was a child he was in a community theater play with Colin Meloy. Also, I listened to a lot of Tarkio in college in Montana and love the Decemberists. So I came to this as a fan of Meloy and was excited to hear his take on an "American Narnia without all the Jesus" as I've heard it described.<br /><br />But ye gods, what a snoozefest this turned out to be. I hated, hated, hated the main characters. I know this is supposed to be a fantasy, but seriously. What upper middle class Portland parents are letting their 12 year old pull their baby around in an open wagon hooked to a bicycle? And then she leaves the baby outside when she goes into the library? I'm pretty sure that is an instant phone call to Child Protective Services these days. And then your 12 year old is suddenly caring for the baby all day and putting him to bed and you don't even see him don't you as a parent just get a teensy bit suspicious?<br /><br />Anyway, I gave it a go but it was just so boring and I was incredibly put off by the adventure set-up at the beginning. It's a little love song to Portland, I guess, and the illustrations are charming but I couldn't get through it.</span>

  <span id="freeText6955320787584399078" style="display:none">This book is a promising start to a series, introducing a magic wood just outside Portland—a wood with talking animals, sentient trees, coyote soldiers, corrupt governments and highway bandits. It is fascinating and imaginative, with a fairytale-ish quality for MG readers, as well as twists creepy and dark enough for adult readers. The plot is not super complicated, as this book is about introducing the world, but the solutions didn’t feel too convenient, and the plot is great in that it’s unpredictable.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer171192635" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5990829105780968437">Really excellent. A proper review to come.<br/><br/><br/>So, proper review - my main, shameful, criteria, for liking anything goes something like this: if I wish to have written, created, taken photograph, worn that item of clothing or thought of that - if I am a little bit jealous of not having come up with the idea - if I want to be the writer and the main character - that's what I love.<br/>Wildwood made me feel both wistful for being twelve and reading it and pretending to be Prue and going on my own adventures</span>
  <span id="freeText5990829105780968437" style="display:none">Really excellent. A proper review to come.<br /><br /><br />So, proper review - my main, shameful, criteria, for liking anything goes something like this: if I wish to have written, created, taken photograph, worn that item of clothing or thought of that - if I am a little bit jealous of not having come up with the idea - if I want to be the writer and the main character - that's what I love.<br />Wildwood made me feel both wistful for being twelve and reading it and pretending to be Prue and going on my own adventures, and also wistful for being Colin Meloy and Carson Ellis, wishing I had made it - the book is so beautiful.<br />Yes, there is sometimes a feeling that you've maybe read parts of the book before. Yes, it made me think of The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe - but you have to understand that I LOOOOVED that book - read it until the cover fell off - I still remember finding it in the library for the first time - it's seminal in my world view - and the fact that Wildwood reminded me of it actually made me like it more.<br /><br />Get it for your 12 year old, for your 12 year old self.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5990829105780968437" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer773345958" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer644220490484476773">The ways in which this book is not for me are numberless, infinite, and really big. The words in this book which could have been eradicated, eliminated, removed and erased without marring the plot are legion, a host, plus a lot. The love Meloy has for helping words, adjectives, and descriptors is prodigious and bountiful. The animals wear clothes for no discernible reason. Plus they use weapons and tools and pencils and other things which require either opposable thumbs or some magical explanati</span>
  <span id="freeText644220490484476773" style="display:none">The ways in which this book is not for me are numberless, infinite, and really big. The words in this book which could have been eradicated, eliminated, removed and erased without marring the plot are legion, a host, plus a lot. The love Meloy has for helping words, adjectives, and descriptors is prodigious and bountiful. The animals wear clothes for no discernible reason. Plus they use weapons and tools and pencils and other things which require either opposable thumbs or some magical explanation. The parents' cavalier treatment of the children in this book defies reason. The plot is both stale and nonsensical. The illustrations didn't resonate with me, save the one of the St. Johns bridge, which bridge I love in any guise. I stuck it out because they are local, and the buzz around here has been huge. I kept hoping it would improve, or I would. Sadly, tragically, unhappily, it did not. <br /></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13716524-evelina-avalinahsbooks?shelf=books-of-2018">books-of-2018</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2504807209" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6720208128766056274">This was a delightful story! It's such a satisfying read - long, but easy to finish, great for both an adult or a child to read. I absolutely loved the world of Wildwood - a little like the society you see in Wind in the Willows - talking animals, living alongside humans. I absolutely love those kinds of stories. Another thing I liked a lot is that it includes both a girl and a boy, and spends roughly the same amount of time on these two characters - a wonderful example of inclusion, as well as </span>
  <span id="freeText6720208128766056274" style="display:none">This was a delightful story! It's such a satisfying read - long, but easy to finish, great for both an adult or a child to read. I absolutely loved the world of Wildwood - a little like the society you see in Wind in the Willows - talking animals, living alongside humans. I absolutely love those kinds of stories. Another thing I liked a lot is that it includes both a girl and a boy, and spends roughly the same amount of time on these two characters - a wonderful example of inclusion, as well as an easy way of making it accessible to both boys and girls, making it relatable and easy to read for everyone. It's a great coming of age story - in their own ways for both the girl as well as the boy character. In general, Wildwood is a lovely and very cozy tale that doesn't lack adventure and thrill even despite being cozy like that. I'll definitely be reading part two!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13263434290503510552">I sort of liked it, ish. It wasn't super good but it was better than ok (edit: it's not). Well-written enough, but Pru (the main character) is just not likeable and I wanted to see her character change and become less of a brat and a know-it-all. No wonder she doesn't have any friends in school if she's always correcting her parents and telling them it was a <i>murder</i> of crows, not a <i>flock</i>.<br/><br/>Here are all the reasons I didn't like the book.<br/><br/>1. I know parents put their kids in charge of their siblings. </span>
  <span id="freeText13263434290503510552" style="display:none">I sort of liked it, ish. It wasn't super good but it was better than ok (edit: it's not). Well-written enough, but Pru (the main character) is just not likeable and I wanted to see her character change and become less of a brat and a know-it-all. No wonder she doesn't have any friends in school if she's always correcting her parents and telling them it was a <i>murder</i> of crows, not a <i>flock</i>.<br /><br />Here are all the reasons I didn't like the book.<br /><br />1. I know parents put their kids in charge of their siblings. Weird, but I know some people do it. However, I don't think I know anyone who puts their kids in charge of their siblings <i>all day</i>. Really? The mom doesn't work, as far as I know, so why does she need a permanent babysitter? And why does Pru never have homework?<br /><br />2. I know they live in Portland, and they're hipster sort of, but would you be ok with leaving your baby brother outside while you slip into a library to drop off your books?<br /><br />3. Dumb that she doesn't even care about the overdue library book. Girl. CUT YOUR LOSSES. You're just racking up the price by not ever dropping it off.<br /><br />4. Ridiculous that the parents would just be like dude ok go for it - go try to find Mac and risk your life. I find it slightly odd that her dad would try to convince her (to stay) and then, when he sees that she's having none of it, he looks sad and watches her leave. Huh? What? And she like "bye, dad" and pedals away and he's just standing there, like ???<br /><br />5. (for those of you who don't want to spoil it for yourselves but do want to know what #5 is, it's a point in the story that is highly unrealistic and unlikely, because of the age of the person who's making a bad decision that s/he wouldn't make irl) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Why does Curtis decide to stay with the bandits and become one of them? What? Don't you miss your family? If I went into the woods and found people I really liked and clicked with, I wouldn't say hey I'll just stay here. Uh no I would say hey I like you guys, you cool, but I got family so see ya bye. AND I'D LEAVE. Sure, I'd try to visit a lot but I wouldn't ditch my family. Because <i>it's my family</i>. It seemed really unbelievable and unrealistic (but just WAIT until you get to the third). <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />In summary, it's an extremely unrealistic book that has some annoying characters and/or stupid characters who aren't worth your time.<br /><br />And there are other things I could complain about but I'm not going to because they're small things and I feel bad that the book is rated an average of 3.65 stars so I'm done. 😛</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5136858-bonnie?shelf=series-graveyard">series-graveyard</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer368717122" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2651693118153759755"><i>'We are the inheritors of a wonderful world, a beautiful world, full of life and mystery, goodness and pain. But likewise are we the children of an indifferent universe. We break our own hearts imposing our moral order on what is, by nature, a wide web of chaos.'</i><br/><br/>Sometimes I wish I didn't give out star-ratings and only wrote reviews, I think sometimes that would be easier than feeling it necessary to justify a low rating despite the fact that I DID like it. But there were some big problems I had </span>
  <span id="freeText2651693118153759755" style="display:none"><i>'We are the inheritors of a wonderful world, a beautiful world, full of life and mystery, goodness and pain. But likewise are we the children of an indifferent universe. We break our own hearts imposing our moral order on what is, by nature, a wide web of chaos.'</i><br /><br />Sometimes I wish I didn't give out star-ratings and only wrote reviews, I think sometimes that would be easier than feeling it necessary to justify a low rating despite the fact that I DID like it. But there were some big problems I had overall.<br /><br />Wildwood is almost a Chronicles of Narnia and Labyrinth mix (minus the fact that Prue didn't wish her brother away). Full of crazy talking animals and a mysterious world known as Wildwood, or as the locals call it, I.W.: Impassable Wilderness.<br /><br /><i>You see, on every map of Portland, Oregon, there is a big splotch of green on the edge of the city labeled I.W. This stands for Impassable Wilderness. No one’s ever gone in—or at least returned to tell of it.'</i><br /><br />The writing flowed, the storyline was entertaining, the small artistic bits strewn throughout were perfection, but...<br /><br />This thing was far too damn long. I may not be a patient reader but still, I know when a book is <i>unnecessarily</i> long. Truly, are there any actual middle-graders out there that read this in its entirety? I would really like to know. For the target audience, middle-graders, I think this would end up being far too much to handle. Extremely political and quite wordy at times (plus, we can't forget the length...541 pages was not necessary). <br /><br />Despite this it was a lovely story and I will continue reading the trilogy (even though that's just as damn long). So readers beware: an extremely enjoyable story, just requires some much needed patience to get through.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2651693118153759755" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_146810266" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/146810266" />
    <a title="Chloe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70078-chloe"><img alt="Chloe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580564408p2/70078.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/146810266?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chloe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chloe" href="/user/show/70078-chloe">Chloe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=scifi-fantasy">scifi-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=not-owned">not-owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=young_adults">young_adults</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=pacific-northwest">pacific-northwest</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=urban_fantasy">urban_fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer146810266" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1496173844893910310">It should really come as no surprise that Colin Meloy would one day turn his spirited imagination to the world of young adult fiction. He has been crafting some of the most exquisitely lyrical songs in rock for over a decade now, from <a href='http://youtu.be/AT_sPE4ahJk' rel='nofollow noopener'>rowsings descriptions of an opulent caravan's entry into a city</a> and <a href='http://youtu.be/Fb54zd_c1Zo' rel='nofollow noopener'>odes to America's most famous spy</a> to lengthy epics about <a href='http://youtu.be/QuEQxhP-Zmo' rel='nofollow noopener'>bandits on mist-wrapped islands</a> and <a href='http://youtu.be/CaNV7eCU2R0' rel='nofollow noopener'>revenge tales to put Monte Cristo to shame</a>. With a mind so firmly rooted in grand tales of picaresque ch</span>
  <span id="freeText1496173844893910310" style="display:none">It should really come as no surprise that Colin Meloy would one day turn his spirited imagination to the world of young adult fiction. He has been crafting some of the most exquisitely lyrical songs in rock for over a decade now, from <a href="http://youtu.be/AT_sPE4ahJk" rel="nofollow noopener">rowsings descriptions of an opulent caravan's entry into a city</a> and <a href="http://youtu.be/Fb54zd_c1Zo" rel="nofollow noopener">odes to America's most famous spy</a> to lengthy epics about <a href="http://youtu.be/QuEQxhP-Zmo" rel="nofollow noopener">bandits on mist-wrapped islands</a> and <a href="http://youtu.be/CaNV7eCU2R0" rel="nofollow noopener">revenge tales to put Monte Cristo to shame</a>. With a mind so firmly rooted in grand tales of picaresque characters, this transition would seem an obvious one. It can work to play to your strengths, as it most assuredly did here.<br /><br />When Prue's baby brother Mac is abducted by a murder of crows and flown deep into the dark of Wildwood, an untamed wood on the other side of the river from Prue's home in the North Portland hamlet of St. John's, she embarks upon a quest most perilous to see him safely returned. With her schoolmate Curtis tagging along, Prue breaches the barrier of the Wildwood and enters a magical world of talking animals, bloodthirsty ivy, and dastardly crooks aplenty. Racing to stop a deposed governess from destroying all of Wildwood, Prue and Curtis are aided by a veritable army of allies- from the Bandit King, Brendan, and the king of avian territory, Owl Rex, to the kindly hippy mystics of North Wood and the ever-hungry rat, Severus. <br /><br />The entire time I was reading this book I was reminded of the stories that my father used to craft for me each night before bed- a huge sprawling epic that continued serially each night for years. While reading <i>Wildwood</i> I could picture Meloy weaving just such a tale for his son, transforming Portland's Forest Park into the magical land of Wildwood, and sending Prue and Curtis on one adventure after another with that wondrous theatric mind of his. Wildwood is set to have two follow-up stories and I already know that I'm going to be reading them as well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1496173844893910310" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_870779285" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/870779285" />
    <a title="Cheri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/106692-cheri"><img alt="Cheri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601402708p2/106692.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/870779285?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheri" href="/user/show/106692-cheri">Cheri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670075157" href="/book/show/12684456-wildwood">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=read-in-2014">read-in-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=fantasy-fiction">fantasy-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer870779285" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12960925903419854544">I’m not a big Decemberist’s fan, although I know who they are, have heard some of their music, but it had nothing to do with my decision to read this book. <br/><br/>I suspect that this book is a must-read for any Portland area residents, It does require a high degree of leniency and a stretch of reality – but then again it isn’t meant to be a non-fiction book. A lot of people seem to have trouble with Prue’s character, but I read this largely because she reminded me a lot of the eldest of the youngest gr</span>
  <span id="freeText12960925903419854544" style="display:none">I’m not a big Decemberist’s fan, although I know who they are, have heard some of their music, but it had nothing to do with my decision to read this book. <br /><br />I suspect that this book is a must-read for any Portland area residents, It does require a high degree of leniency and a stretch of reality – but then again it isn’t meant to be a non-fiction book. A lot of people seem to have trouble with Prue’s character, but I read this largely because she reminded me a lot of the eldest of the youngest group in our family. Precocious, adorable and with a bit of temper when she doesn’t get things her way, but you know when it comes down to it she would cross mountains to save her family. So, I don’t have any problem believing that children like Prue exist, because I know they do. <br /><br />This is a fantasy fiction book that is lengthy, and there are brief moments where I think it was lacking or perhaps just dragging, but ultimately I felt it was worth the read. <br /><br />I wish there were more books like this for that age group who need more growing-independent-childhood books, but aren't quite ready for (or whose parents aren't ready for them to read what comes with) the next level of fantasy fiction brings with it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12960925903419854544" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_182144007" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/182144007" />
    <a title="Adam Silvera" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4050629-adam-silvera"><img alt="Adam Silvera" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595433626p2/4050629.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/182144007?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam Silvera" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam Silvera" href="/user/show/4050629-adam-silvera">Adam Silvera</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4050629-adam-silvera?shelf=bea-2011">bea-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4050629-adam-silvera?shelf=70-reads-for-2012">70-reads-for-2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer182144007" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3229075412561035350">Recommended for readers who are looking to take a stroll through Wildwood who won't mind stopping to smell every rose, so to speak. Meloy's inspiration of the wood in Portland is captured vividly and illustrated dutifully by Ellis. This book's also for the reader who enjoys little episodes that may not necessarily impact the story, but adds an extra spark between the pages. The story felt complete and left enough up in the air to answer with its upcoming sequel which I'll certainly pick up.<br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('nqE+6quxOtQPLXPJMguxG5YCKczOc6aru3RJiKSdujsPTtZik2Ud/wZFm+x95+p2iy8viHQKtRr8xReCdzUO3g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('xgdFZDqvu1rsFHhlWe6uQV8MhfV9xFJga5/N35XL60pX6K3sAnucceV8kEAWAvUsQiGDsce9QdEsLpPVRmNfrw==')})
  }

As part of a plan to save money, government officials decided that Flint would temporarily switch its water supply from Lake Huron to the Flint River. Within months, many residents broke out in rashes. Then it got worse: children stopped growing. Some people were hospitalized with mysterious illnesses; others died. Citizens of Flint protested that the water was dangerous. Despite what seemed so apparent from the murky, foul-smelling liquid pouring from the city\'s faucets, officials refused to listen. They treated the people of Flint as the problem, not the water, which was actually poisoning thousands.Through interviews with residents and intensive research into legal records and news accounts, journalist Candy J. Cooper, assisted by writer-editor Marc Aronson, reveals the true story of Flint. Poisoned Water shows not just how the crisis unfolded in 2014, but also the history of racism and segregation that led up to it, the beliefs and attitudes that fueled it, and how the people of Flint fought--and are still fighting--for clean water and healthy lives.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"7745838407071930333\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n Before he got the chance to find out, however, an automobile accident in Ireland left him with the lasting wounds of body and spirit that ultimately led him to take his own life. But as his dear friend novelist Francine Prose notes in this volume\'s foreword, &quot;Even when his depression became so severe that he was barely able to hold a simple conversation, he was, miraculously, able to perform.&quot;\n\nAs was always his method, Gray began to fashion a new monologue in various workshop settings that would tell the story of the accident and its aftermath. Originally titled Black Spot--for what the locals called the section of highway where Gray\'s accident occurred--it began as a series of workshops at P.S. 122 in New York City and eventually became Life Interrupted.Gray died in early 2004, and though never completed, Life Interrupted is rich with brave self-revelation, masterfully acute observations of wonderfully peculiar people, penetrating wit and genuine humor, an irresolvable fascination with life and death, and all the other attributes of Gray\'s singular and unmistakable voice.\n\nIn the final performance of Life Interrupted, Gray read two additional pieces: a short story about a day he spent with his son Theo at the carousel in Central Park and a brief, poignant love letter to New York City that he wrote after the terrorist attacks in 2001. This volume includes these pieces as well as many of the eulogies that were delivered by his friends and family at memorial services held at Lincoln Center and in Sag Harbor.\n\n\n\n[If you had to reduce all of Spalding\'s work to its essence, its core, if you wanted to locate the subject to which, no matter what else he talked about, he kept returning, I suppose you could say that his work was a profoundly metaphysical inquiry into how we manage to live despite the knowledge that we are someday going to die. . . .\n\nIf there is a consolation, it\'s what he left behind: the children whom he so loved and, of course, his work. Reading the unfinished pieces in this volume . . . we hear his voice again and feel the happiness we felt when he sat on stage behind his wooden desk, took a sip from his water glass, transformed the raw material of his life into art, and the crowd applauded each brilliant, beautiful sentence.] --Francine Prose, from the Foreword\n\nAlso available as an eBook<\/span>\n  <a <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer390317442131754750">Colin Patrick Henry Meloy is the lead singer and songwriter for the Portland, Oregon folk-rock band The Decemberists. In addition to his vocal duties, he plays acoustic guitar, 12-string acoustic guitar, electric guitar, bouzouki, and percussion. As of 2005, Meloy has written a 100-page book on The Replacements' fourth album, "Let It Be," released as part of the 33⅓ series.<br/><br/>Meloy was born in Helena</span>
  <span id="freeText390317442131754750" style="display:none">Colin Patrick Henry Meloy is the lead singer and songwriter for the Portland, Oregon folk-rock band The Decemberists. In addition to his vocal duties, he plays acoustic guitar, 12-string acoustic guitar, electric guitar, bouzouki, and percussion. As of 2005, Meloy has written a 100-page book on The Replacements' fourth album, "Let It Be," released as part of the 33⅓ series.<br /><br />Meloy was born in Helena, Montana. His sister is Maile Meloy, an author often published in <i>The New Yorker</i>. He first attended the University of Oregon before transferring to the University of Montana, where he majored in creative writing. He then moved to Portland, where he met future bandmates Jenny Conlee and Nate Query. Prior to being in The Decemberists, Meloy was the lead singer and songwriter of Happy Cactus and Tarkio, both indie/folk bands from Montana.<br /><br />In early 2005, he embarked on his first solo tour in support of the self-released six-song EP, "Colin Meloy Sings Morrissey," which consists of six Morrissey covers. Only 1,000 copies of the album were made, and they were sold only on that tour. Meloy did a second solo tour in January 2006, playing with Laura Veirs and Amy Annelle. On this tour, he sold an EP featuring covers of British folk artist Shirley Collins. Shows from the 2006 tour were recorded for a live release. Meloy also appeared with Charlie Salas-Humara in the music video for "Pillar of Salt" by The Thermals.<br /><br />On February 24, 2006, Carson Ellis, Meloy's longtime girlfriend and graphic artist for the Decemberists, gave birth to their son, Henry "Hank" Meloy.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="390317442131754750" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

Her mind is constantly returning to the verdant groves and sky-tall trees of Wildwood, where her friend Curtis still remains as a bandit-in-training.\n\nBut all is not well in that world.
